# Title: Applied Economics: Theory and Applications

## Chapter 4: Microeconomics




### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 4 of "Applied Economics: Theory and Applications"! In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of international economics. International economics is a branch of economics that deals with the study of economic relationships between different countries. It is a crucial aspect of modern economics, as it helps us understand the complex dynamics of the global economy.

In this chapter, we will explore the various theories and applications of international economics. We will start by discussing the basics of international trade and how it affects the economies of different countries. We will then move on to more advanced topics such as exchange rates, international finance, and international trade agreements.

One of the key concepts in international economics is the concept of comparative advantage. This concept helps us understand why countries engage in international trade and how it benefits them. We will also discuss the different types of trade agreements, such as free trade agreements and customs unions, and their impact on international trade.

Furthermore, we will explore the role of international organizations, such as the International Monetary Fund and the World Bank, in regulating and promoting international trade. We will also discuss the challenges and controversies surrounding international trade, such as the impact of globalization on developing countries and the role of protectionism in international trade.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the principles and applications of international economics. You will also be able to critically analyze and evaluate the current state of the global economy and its future prospects. So let's dive into the world of international economics and discover the fascinating dynamics of the global economy.




### Subsection: 4.1a Comparative Advantage and Gains from Trade

Comparative advantage is a fundamental concept in international trade theory. It is the basis for understanding why countries engage in trade and how it benefits them. In this section, we will explore the concept of comparative advantage and its implications for international trade.

#### Comparative Advantage

Comparative advantage is a concept that is closely related to the concept of absolute advantage. Absolute advantage refers to a country's ability to produce a good or service at a lower cost than another country. Comparative advantage, on the other hand, refers to a country's ability to produce a good or service at a lower opportunity cost than another country.

To understand comparative advantage, we must first understand the concept of opportunity cost. Opportunity cost is the cost of choosing one alternative over another. In the context of international trade, opportunity cost refers to the cost of producing a good or service in terms of the resources that could have been used to produce something else.

A country has a comparative advantage in producing a good or service if it has a lower opportunity cost of producing that good or service compared to another country. This means that the country can produce the good or service at a lower cost in terms of the resources that could have been used to produce something else.

#### Gains from Trade

Trade between countries can lead to gains for all parties involved. These gains are known as the gains from trade. The gains from trade occur when countries specialize in producing goods or services that they have a comparative advantage in, and then trade with each other to obtain the goods or services that they do not have a comparative advantage in.

To understand the gains from trade, we must first understand the concept of specialization. Specialization occurs when a country produces a good or service that it has a comparative advantage in, and then trades with another country to obtain the good or service that it does not have a comparative advantage in.

When countries specialize and trade, they can produce more of the goods or services that they have a comparative advantage in, and obtain more of the goods or services that they do not have a comparative advantage in. This leads to increased production and consumption, resulting in gains for all parties involved.

#### Empirical Evidence

Empirical evidence supports the concept of comparative advantage and the gains from trade. MacDougall (1951, 1952) was one of the first to test the relationship between output per worker and the number of exports, and found a positive relationship. This relationship has been replicated by other researchers, such as Stern (1962) and Balassa (1963).

More recent studies, such as Golub and Hsieh (2000) and Nunn (2007), have also found strong correlations between relative productivity and trade patterns. These studies suggest that countries that have greater productivity tend to specialize in goods that require more advanced technology and skills, leading to increased trade and gains for all parties involved.

#### Conclusion

In conclusion, comparative advantage and the gains from trade are fundamental concepts in international trade theory. They explain why countries engage in trade and how it benefits them. Empirical evidence supports these concepts, and they continue to be relevant in understanding the dynamics of the global economy. In the next section, we will explore the different types of trade agreements and their impact on international trade.





### Subsection: 4.1b Trade Policies

Trade policies play a crucial role in shaping the international trade landscape. They are the set of rules and regulations that govern how countries interact with each other in terms of trade. These policies can have a significant impact on a country's economy, and understanding them is essential for any student of international economics.

#### Types of Trade Policies

There are several types of trade policies that countries can implement. These include tariffs, quotas, and subsidies. Tariffs are taxes imposed on imported goods, while quotas are limits on the amount of a good that can be imported. Subsidies, on the other hand, are financial assistance given to domestic producers to help them compete with imported goods.

#### Trade Policies and Comparative Advantage

Trade policies can have a significant impact on a country's comparative advantage. For instance, tariffs can protect domestic industries from foreign competition, allowing them to maintain their comparative advantage. Quotas, on the other hand, can limit the amount of a good that can be imported, potentially giving domestic industries a monopoly and further enhancing their comparative advantage.

However, trade policies can also have negative effects on comparative advantage. For example, subsidies can distort market prices and encourage overproduction, leading to a loss of comparative advantage. Similarly, tariffs and quotas can limit competition, leading to higher prices and reducing the incentive for domestic industries to become more efficient and competitive.

#### Trade Policies and Gains from Trade

Trade policies can also impact the gains from trade. For instance, tariffs and quotas can reduce the volume of trade, limiting the potential gains from trade. On the other hand, policies that promote fair competition, such as anti-dumping measures, can help ensure that all countries benefit from trade.

#### Trade Policies and International Cooperation

Finally, trade policies can also impact international cooperation. For instance, the Transatlantic Trade and Investment Partnership (TTIP) is an example of two countries working together to reduce trade barriers and promote fair competition. Similarly, the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT) and the World Trade Organization (WTO) are international organizations that aim to promote fair and transparent trade practices.

In conclusion, trade policies play a crucial role in shaping the international trade landscape. Understanding these policies is essential for any student of international economics, as they can have a significant impact on a country's economy and its interactions with other countries.




### Subsection: 4.1c Trade Agreements and Organizations

Trade agreements and organizations play a crucial role in shaping the international trade landscape. They provide a framework for countries to negotiate and implement trade policies, and they also serve as platforms for resolving trade disputes. In this section, we will explore some of the key trade agreements and organizations that have shaped international trade.

#### The World Trade Organization (WTO)

The World Trade Organization (WTO) is the primary international organization for regulating global trade. It was established in 1995 as a successor to the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT). The WTO sets rules for global trade, monitors trade policies, and provides a forum for countries to negotiate trade agreements. It also has a dispute resolution mechanism to resolve trade disputes.

The WTO has been instrumental in promoting free trade and reducing barriers to trade. It has also been a platform for developing countries to voice their concerns and negotiate for better trade terms. However, the WTO has also faced criticism for its lack of transparency and its failure to address issues such as intellectual property rights and environmental standards.

#### The North American Free Trade Agreement (NAFTA)

The North American Free Trade Agreement (NAFTA) is a trade agreement between the United States, Canada, and Mexico. It was signed in 1994 and has been a major driver of trade between these three countries. NAFTA has led to a significant increase in trade, with total trade between the three countries reaching over $1 billion per day in 2017.

However, NAFTA has also faced criticism for its impact on jobs and wages in the United States. Some studies have found that NAFTA has led to job losses in certain industries, particularly in manufacturing. However, other studies have found that NAFTA has had a positive impact on the overall U.S. economy.

#### The European Union (EU)

The European Union (EU) is a political and economic union of 27 member states. It has been a major driver of trade within Europe and has also been a major player in global trade. The EU has implemented a common external tariff, which has helped to create a single market for goods and services. It has also implemented a common agricultural policy, which has helped to ensure food security for its members.

The EU has also been a major advocate for free trade and has been instrumental in negotiating trade agreements with other countries. However, the EU has also faced criticism for its protectionist policies, particularly in the areas of agriculture and intellectual property rights.

#### The United States-Mexico-Canada Agreement (USMCA)

The United States-Mexico-Canada Agreement (USMCA) is a trade agreement that was signed in 2018 to replace NAFTA. The USMCA includes updated provisions on trade, labor, and environmental standards. It also includes provisions for dispute resolution and intellectual property rights.

The USMCA has been a major focus of trade negotiations in recent years, with the United States, Mexico, and Canada engaging in lengthy negotiations to reach an agreement. The USMCA has been praised for its updated provisions, but it has also faced criticism for its lack of provisions on certain issues, such as climate change.

In conclusion, trade agreements and organizations play a crucial role in shaping the international trade landscape. They provide a framework for countries to negotiate and implement trade policies, and they also serve as platforms for resolving trade disputes. The WTO, NAFTA, the EU, and the USMCA are just a few examples of the many trade agreements and organizations that have shaped international trade.





### Subsection: 4.2a Exchange Rates

Exchange rates play a crucial role in international trade and finance. They determine the value of one country's currency in terms of another country's currency. In this section, we will explore the different types of exchange rate regimes and their implications for international trade and finance.

#### Fixed Exchange Rate Regime

A fixed exchange rate regime is a system where a country's currency is pegged to a specific value, usually to a major currency such as the US dollar. This regime is often used by countries to maintain a stable exchange rate and reduce exchange rate volatility. However, it also limits a country's ability to adjust its exchange rate in response to economic shocks.

One example of a country with a fixed exchange rate regime is Hong Kong. The Hong Kong dollar is pegged to the US dollar at a rate of HK$7.8 to US$1. This has helped to maintain a stable exchange rate and attract foreign investment. However, it has also limited Hong Kong's ability to adjust its exchange rate in response to economic shocks, such as the 2008 global financial crisis.

#### Floating Exchange Rate Regime

A floating exchange rate regime is a system where a country's currency is allowed to float freely in the foreign exchange market. This regime allows a country's exchange rate to be determined by market forces, such as supply and demand. Floating exchange rates can help to reduce exchange rate volatility in the long run, but they can also lead to short-term fluctuations.

One example of a country with a floating exchange rate regime is the United States. The US dollar is the world's reserve currency, and its exchange rate is influenced by a variety of factors, including interest rates, economic growth, and investor sentiment. The floating exchange rate regime has allowed the US dollar to appreciate significantly in recent years, making it more expensive for other countries to purchase US goods and services.

#### Managed Float Exchange Rate Regime

A managed float exchange rate regime is a system where a country's currency is allowed to float freely, but the government intervenes in the foreign exchange market to influence its exchange rate. This regime combines elements of both fixed and floating exchange rate regimes.

One example of a country with a managed float exchange rate regime is Japan. The Japanese government has been intervening in the foreign exchange market to prevent the yen from appreciating, as a stronger yen can make Japanese exports less competitive. However, the effectiveness of this intervention has been debated, and some argue that it has led to a distortion of market forces.

#### Implications for International Trade and Finance

The choice of exchange rate regime can have significant implications for international trade and finance. A fixed exchange rate regime can help to maintain a stable exchange rate, but it can also limit a country's ability to adjust its exchange rate in response to economic shocks. A floating exchange rate regime can help to reduce exchange rate volatility in the long run, but it can also lead to short-term fluctuations. A managed float exchange rate regime combines elements of both, but it can also lead to market distortions.

In addition, the choice of exchange rate regime can also impact a country's trade competitiveness. A country with a strong currency, such as the US, can make its exports more expensive and less competitive in the global market. On the other hand, a country with a weak currency, such as Argentina, can make its exports more affordable and increase its trade competitiveness.

Overall, the choice of exchange rate regime is a crucial decision for any country, and it can have significant implications for its economy and international trade and finance. 





### Subsection: 4.2b Balance of Payments

The balance of payments (BoP) is a crucial concept in international finance. It is a systematic record of all the economic transactions between a country and the rest of the world in a given time period. These transactions include payments for goods, services, financial capital, and financial transfers. The BoP is prepared in a single currency, typically the domestic currency for the country concerned.

The BoP accounts are maintained by double entry bookkeeping, which ensures that they always balance. Sources of funds for a nation, such as exports or the receipts of loans and investments, are recorded as positive or surplus items. Uses of funds, such as imports or to invest in foreign countries, are recorded as negative or deficit items.

When all components of the BoP accounts are included, they must sum to zero with no overall surplus or deficit. For example, if a country is importing more than it exports, its trade balance will be in deficit, but the shortfall will have to be counterbalanced in other ways – such as by funds earned from its foreign investments (but not the investments themselves, since foreign investments are deficit items), by running down currency reserves or by receiving investments or loans from other countries.

However, imbalances are possible on individual elements of the BoP, such as the current account, the capital account excluding the central bank's reserve account, or the sum of the two. Imbalances in the latter sum can result in surplus countries accumulating wealth, while deficit countries may experience a decline in their wealth.

The BoP is a crucial tool for understanding a country's international financial position. It provides insights into the country's trade performance, its ability to attract foreign investment, and its dependence on external financing. It also helps to identify potential sources of instability in the economy, such as a persistent current account deficit.

In the next section, we will explore the components of the BoP in more detail and discuss their implications for a country's international financial position.




### Subsection: 4.2c International Monetary System

The international monetary system is a complex network of agreements, institutions, and practices that facilitate the flow of goods, services, and capital across national borders. It is designed to promote international trade and investment, and to manage international financial transactions. The system is governed by a set of rules and conventions that are agreed upon by the member countries of the International Monetary Fund (IMF).

The international monetary system is composed of two main components: the international monetary system proper, and the international financial system. The international monetary system proper includes the international monetary institutions, such as the IMF and the World Bank, and the international monetary agreements, such as the Bretton Woods Agreement and the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT). The international financial system includes the international financial institutions, such as the International Bank for Reconstruction and Development (IBRD) and the International Development Association (IDA), and the international financial agreements, such as the International Monetary Fund Agreement and the World Bank Agreement.

The international monetary system is also characterized by the use of international currencies, such as the US dollar, the euro, and the Japanese yen, which are used as reserve currencies and as units of account in international trade and finance. These currencies are often referred to as "hard currencies" because they are considered to be stable and reliable.

The international monetary system is not without its challenges. One of the main challenges is the management of international financial imbalances. These imbalances can arise from differences in economic growth rates, differences in interest rates, and differences in exchange rate policies. They can also arise from differences in fiscal policies, such as government spending and taxation policies.

The international monetary system is also subject to various risks, such as the risk of currency crises, the risk of financial crises, and the risk of economic crises. These risks can be exacerbated by the interconnectedness of the global economy, which can lead to contagion effects.

Despite these challenges and risks, the international monetary system continues to play a crucial role in promoting international trade and investment, and in managing international financial transactions. It is a key component of the global economy, and its effectiveness is essential for the continued growth and stability of the world economy.




### Subsection: 4.3a Customs Union and Free Trade Area

Customs unions and free trade areas are two key forms of economic integration that have been widely adopted by countries around the world. These arrangements are designed to promote trade and economic growth by reducing barriers to trade and facilitating the movement of goods, services, and capital across national borders.

#### Customs Union

A customs union is a form of economic integration in which member countries agree to eliminate tariffs and other barriers to trade on a wide range of goods. This means that goods produced in any member country can enter any other member country without facing any trade barriers. The European Union is a prime example of a customs union, with its members agreeing to eliminate tariffs and other barriers to trade on a wide range of goods.

In addition to eliminating tariffs, customs unions often also include provisions for common external tariffs. This means that member countries agree to apply the same tariffs to imports from non-member countries. This is designed to prevent non-member countries from taking advantage of differences in tariffs between member countries to avoid paying tariffs.

#### Free Trade Area

A free trade area is a form of economic integration in which member countries agree to eliminate tariffs and other barriers to trade on a limited range of goods. Unlike a customs union, a free trade area does not require member countries to eliminate tariffs on all goods. Instead, member countries agree to eliminate tariffs only on a specific list of goods. The North American Free Trade Agreement (NAFTA) is an example of a free trade area.

One of the key differences between a customs union and a free trade area is that a customs union also includes provisions for common external tariffs. This means that member countries agree to apply the same tariffs to imports from non-member countries. In contrast, a free trade area does not require member countries to apply the same tariffs to imports from non-member countries. This can lead to what is known as "tariff jumping", where non-member countries can avoid paying tariffs by routing their imports through a member country with lower tariffs.

#### Comparison of Customs Union and Free Trade Area

Both customs unions and free trade areas have their advantages and disadvantages. Customs unions are generally more comprehensive, as they require member countries to eliminate tariffs on a wide range of goods. This can lead to increased trade and economic growth. However, customs unions can also lead to what is known as "tariff jumping", where non-member countries can avoid paying tariffs by routing their imports through a member country with lower tariffs.

Free trade areas, on the other hand, are less comprehensive. They only require member countries to eliminate tariffs on a limited range of goods. This can make it easier for member countries to join a free trade area, as they do not have to eliminate tariffs on all goods. However, this can also limit the benefits of free trade, as non-member countries can still avoid paying tariffs by routing their imports through a member country with lower tariffs.

In conclusion, both customs unions and free trade areas have their advantages and disadvantages. The choice between these two forms of economic integration depends on the specific circumstances and goals of the countries involved.




### Subsection: 4.3b Economic and Monetary Union

An economic and monetary union (EMU) is a type of trade bloc that features a combination of a common market, customs union, and monetary union. Established via a trade pact, an EMU constitutes the sixth of seven stages in the process of economic integration. An EMU agreement usually combines a customs union with a common market. A typical EMU establishes free trade and a common external tariff throughout its jurisdiction. It is also designed to protect freedom in the movement of goods, services, and people. This arrangement is distinct from a monetary union (e.g., the Latin Monetary Union), which does not usually involve a common market. As with the economic and monetary union established among the 27 member states of the European Union (EU), an EMU may affect different parts of its jurisdiction in different ways. Some areas are subject to separate customs regulations from other areas subject to the EMU. These various arrangements may be established in a formal agreement, or they may exist on a "de facto" basis. For example, not all EU member states use the Euro established by its currency union, and not all EU member states are part of the Schengen Area. Some EU members participate in both unions, and some in neither.

Territories of the United States, Australian External Territories and New Zealand territories each share a currency and, for the most part, the market of their respective mainland states. However, they are generally not part of the same customs territories.

#### 4.3b.1 History

Several countries initially attempted to form an EMU at the Hague Summit in 1969. Afterward, a "draft plan" was announced. During this time, the main member presiding over this decision was Pierre Werner, Prime Minister of Luxembourg. The decision to form the Economic and Monetary Union of the European Union (EMU) was accepted in December 1991, which later became part of the Maastricht Treaty (the Treaty on European Union).

#### 4.3b.2 Economic and Monetary Union in the European Union

The European Union (EU) is a prime example of an economic and monetary union. The EU has a common market, customs union, and currency union. The EU's common market allows for free trade and movement of goods, services, and people among its member states. The customs union establishes a common external tariff for all member states. The currency union, established by the Euro, is used by 19 of the 27 EU member states.

The EMU in the EU has been a significant factor in the economic integration of its member states. It has led to increased trade and investment, as well as improved economic stability. However, it has also faced challenges, such as the Greek debt crisis and the ongoing Brexit negotiations.

#### 4.3b.3 Economic and Monetary Union in Other Regions

While the EU is the most prominent example of an economic and monetary union, there are other regions that have also established EMUs. For example, the Caribbean Community (CARICOM) has a common market and customs union, but not a monetary union. The Southern African Development Community (SADC) has a common market and customs union, but not a monetary union. These EMUs have varying degrees of integration and face their own unique challenges and opportunities.

In conclusion, economic and monetary union is a crucial aspect of international economics. It allows for increased trade and investment, as well as improved economic stability. However, it also presents challenges that must be addressed to ensure its success.
